</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Methane</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="/θ/: 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="/θ/: 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<sub>4</sub></span> (one atom of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and four atoms of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>). It is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group-14_hydride" title="Group-14 hydride">group-14 hydride</a> and the simplest <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkane</a>, and is the main constituent of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. The relative abundance of methane on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> makes it an attractive <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a>, though capturing and storing it poses challenges due to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaseous" title="Gaseous">gaseous</a> state under <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">normal conditions for temperature and pressure</a>.
</p><p>Natural methane is found both below ground and under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_floor" title="Sea floor">sea floor</a>. When it reaches the surface and the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a>, it is known as <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">atmospheric methane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The Earth's atmospheric methane concentration has increased by about 150% since 1750, and it accounts for 20% of the total <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> from all of the long-lived and globally mixed <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_summary_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Technical_summary-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:163px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alessandro_Volta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="161" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Alessandro_Volta.jpg" width="161"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alessandro_Volta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a></div></div></div>
<p>In November 1776, methane was first scientifically identified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_people" title="Italian people">Italian</a> physicist <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a> in the marshes of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maggiore" title="Lake Maggiore">Lake Maggiore</a> straddling <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>.  Volta was inspired to search for the substance after reading a paper written by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> about "flammable air".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Volta_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Volta-8">[8]</a></sup> Volta collected the gas rising from the marsh, and by 1778 had isolated the pure gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookrags_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-bookrags-9">[9]</a></sup> He also demonstrated that the gas could be ignited with an electric spark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bookrags_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-bookrags-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name "methane" was coined in 1866 by the German chemist <a href="/wiki/August_Wilhelm_von_Hofmann" title="August Wilhelm von Hofmann">August Wilhelm von Hofmann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The name was derived from <a href="/wiki/Methanol#History" title="Methanol">methanol</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Etymologically, the word "<i>methane</i>" is coined from chemical suffix "<i>-ane</i>" which represents the substance belonging to alkanes family and the word "<i>methyl</i>" which is derived from German "<i>methyl</i>" (A.D.1840) or directly from French "<i>méthyle</i>" which is a back-formation from French "<i>méthylène</i>" (of which correspondence in English is "methylene") the root of which is coined from Greek "<i>methy</i>"(of which correspondence in English is "mead") and "<i>hyle</i>" (meaning "wood"). The radical is named after this because it was first detected in wood alcohol. The chemical suffix "<i>-ane</i>" is from the coordinating chemical suffix "<i>-ine</i>" which is from Latin feminine suffix "<i>-ina</i>" which is applied to represent abstracts. The coordination of "-ane", "-ene", "-one", etc. is proposed in 1866 by German chemist August Wilhelm von Hofmann (1818-1892).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_bonding">Properties and bonding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Properties and bonding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Methane is a <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Tetrahedral molecular geometry">tetrahedral</a> molecule with four equivalent <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93hydrogen_bond" title="Carbon–hydrogen bond">C–H bonds</a>. Its electronic structure is described by four bonding molecular orbitals (MOs) resulting from the overlap of the valence orbitals on <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a>. The lowest energy MO is the result of the overlap of the 2s orbital on carbon with the in-phase combination of the 1s orbitals on the four hydrogen atoms. Above this energy level is a triply degenerate set of MOs that involve overlap of the 2p orbitals on carbon with various linear combinations of the 1s orbitals on hydrogen. The resulting "three-over-one" bonding scheme is consistent with photoelectron spectroscopic measurements.
</p><p>At <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_pressure" title="Standard pressure">standard pressure</a>, methane is a colorless, odorless gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The familiar smell of natural gas as used in homes is achieved by the addition of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odorant" title="Odorant">odorant</a>, usually blends containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tert-butylthiol" title="Tert-butylthiol">tert-butylthiol</a>, as a safety measure. Methane has a boiling point of −164 °<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a> (−257.8 °<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">F</a>) at a pressure of one <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> As a gas it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flammable" title="Flammable">flammable</a> over a range of concentrations (5.4–17%) in air at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_pressure" title="Standard pressure">standard pressure</a>.
</p><p>Solid methane exists in several <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">modifications</a>. Presently nine are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiniPratesi_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-BiniPratesi-13">[13]</a></sup> Cooling methane at normal pressure results in the formation of methane I. This substance crystallizes in the cubic system (<a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> Fm<span style="text-decoration:overline;">3</span>m). The positions of the hydrogen atoms are not fixed in methane I, i.e. methane molecules may rotate freely. Therefore, it is a <a href="/wiki/Plastic_crystal" title="Plastic crystal">plastic crystal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_reactions">Chemical reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemical reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The primary chemical reactions of methane are <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a> to <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Halogenation" title="Halogenation">halogenation</a>. In general, methane reactions are difficult to control. Partial oxidation to <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, for example, is challenging because the reaction typically progresses all the way to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> even with an insufficient supply of oxygen. The enzyme <a href="/wiki/Methane_monooxygenase" title="Methane monooxygenase">methane monooxygenase</a> produces methanol from methane, but cannot be used for industrial-scale reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Some homogeneously catalyzed systems and heterogeneous systems have been developed, but all have significant drawbacks. These generally operate by generating protected products which are shielded from overoxidation. Examples include the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Catalytica_system&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Catalytica system (page does not exist)">Catalytica system</a>, copper zeolites, and iron zeolites stabilizing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-Oxygen" title="Alpha-Oxygen">Alpha-Oxygen</a> active site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acid-base_reactions">Acid-base reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Acid-base reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like other <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, methane is a very weak acid. Its pKa in <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfoxide" title="Dimethyl sulfoxide">DMSO</a> is estimated to be 56.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> It cannot be <a href="/wiki/Deprotonation" title="Deprotonation">deprotonated</a> in solution, but the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_base" title="Conjugate base">conjugate base</a> is known in forms such as <a href="/wiki/Methyllithium" title="Methyllithium">methyllithium</a>.
</p><p>A variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">positive ions</a> derived from methane have been observed, mostly as unstable species in low-pressure gas mixtures. These include <a href="/wiki/Methenium" title="Methenium">methenium</a> or methyl cation <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, methane cation <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Methanium" title="Methanium">methanium</a> or protonated methane <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>. Some of these have been <a href="/wiki/List_of_interstellar_and_circumstellar_molecules" title="List of interstellar and circumstellar molecules">detected in outer space</a>. Methanium can also be produced as diluted solutions from methane with <a href="/wiki/Superacid" title="Superacid">superacids</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">Cations</a> with higher charge, such as <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span></span>, have been studied theoretically and conjectured to be stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rasul_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rasul-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the strength of its C–H bonds, there is intense interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalysts" title="Catalysts">catalysts</a> that facilitate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%E2%80%93H_bond_activation" title="C–H bond activation">C–H bond activation</a> in methane (and other lower numbered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkanes" title="Alkanes">alkanes</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion">Combustion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Combustion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_fire_within_her.jpg"><img alt="A young woman holding a flame in her hands" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/The_fire_within_her.jpg/220px-The_fire_within_her.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/The_fire_within_her.jpg/330px-The_fire_within_her.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/The_fire_within_her.jpg/440px-The_fire_within_her.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_fire_within_her.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Methane bubbles can be burned on a wet hand without injury.</div></div></div>
<p>Methane's <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_combustion" title="Heat of combustion">heat of combustion</a> is 55.5 MJ/kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">Combustion</a> of methane is a multiple step reaction summarized as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>4</sub> + 2 O<sub>2</sub> → CO<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O (<span class="nowrap">Δ<i>H</i> = −891 <a href="/wiki/Kilo-" title="Kilo-">k</a><a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a>/<a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mol</a></span>, at standard conditions)</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peters_four-step_chemistry" title="Peters four-step chemistry">Peters four-step chemistry</a> is a systematically reduced four-step chemistry which explains the burning of methane.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_Reactions">Methane Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Methane Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Given appropriate conditions, methane reacts with as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd>X• + CH<sub>4</sub> → HX + CH<sub>3</sub>•</dd>
<dd>CH<sub>3</sub>• + X<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>3</sub>X + X•</dd></dl>
<p>where X is a <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogen</a>: <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> (F), <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> (Cl), <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> (Br), or <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> (I). This mechanism for this process is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical_halogenation" title="Free radical halogenation">free radical halogenation</a>. It is initiated when UV light or some other <a href="/wiki/Radical_initiator" title="Radical initiator">radical initiator</a> produces a halogen <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a>. A two-step <a href="/wiki/Chain_reaction" title="Chain reaction">chain reaction</a> ensues in which the halogen atom abstracts a hydrogen atom from a methane molecule, resulting in the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_halide" title="Hydrogen halide">hydrogen halide</a> molecule and a <a href="/wiki/Methyl_radical" title="Methyl radical">methyl radical</a> (CH<sub>3</sub>•). The methyl radical then reacts with a molecule of the halogen to form a molecule of the halomethane, with a new halogen atom as byproduct. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Similar reactions can occur on the halogenated product, leading to replacement of additional hydrogen atoms by halogen atoms with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihalomethane" title="Dihalomethane">dihalomethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trihalomethane" title="Trihalomethane">trihalomethane</a>, and ultimately, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tetrahlomethane&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tetrahlomethane (page does not exist)">tetrahlomethane</a> structures, depending upon reaction conditions and the halogen-to-methane ratio.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Methane is used in industrial chemical processes and may be transported as a refrigerated liquid (liquefied natural gas, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LNG" title="LNG">LNG</a>). While leaks from a refrigerated liquid container are initially heavier than air due to the increased density of the cold gas, the gas at ambient temperature is lighter than air. <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">Gas pipelines</a> distribute large amounts of natural gas, of which methane is the principal component.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel">Fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methane is used as a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> for ovens, homes, water heaters, kilns, automobiles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> turbines, and other things. <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">Activated carbon</a> is used to store methane.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas">Natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a></div>
<p>Methane is important for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a> by burning it as a fuel in a <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Boiler_(power_generation)" title="Boiler (power generation)">steam generator</a>. Compared to other <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">hydrocarbon fuels</a>, methane produces less <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> for each unit of heat released. At about 891 kJ/mol, methane's <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_combustion" title="Heat of combustion">heat of combustion</a> is lower than any other hydrocarbon but the ratio of the heat of combustion (891 kJ/mol) to the molecular mass (16.0 g/mol, of which 12.0 g/mol is carbon) shows that methane, being the simplest hydrocarbon, produces more heat per mass unit (55.7 kJ/g) than other complex hydrocarbons. In many cities, methane is piped into homes for domestic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heating" title="Heating">heating</a> and cooking. In this context it is usually known as <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, which is considered to have an energy content of 39 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megajoule" title="Megajoule">megajoules</a> per cubic meter, or 1,000 <a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">BTU</a> per <a href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_foot" title="Standard cubic foot">standard cubic foot</a>.
</p><p>Methane in the form of <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> is used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vehicle_fuel" title="Vehicle fuel">vehicle fuel</a> and is claimed to be more environmentally friendly than other fossil fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrol" title="Petrol">petrol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> Research into <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorption</a> methods of methane storage for use as an automotive fuel has been conducted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="856" data-file-width="1588" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg/300px-Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg/450px-Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg/600px-Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Methanier_aspher_LNGRIVERS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Expensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LNG_tanker" title="LNG tanker">LNG tankers</a> are required to transport methane.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquefied_natural_gas">Liquefied natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Liquefied natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">Liquefied natural gas</a></div>
<p>Liquefied natural gas (LNG) is <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> (predominantly methane, CH<sub>4</sub>) that has been converted to liquid form for ease of storage or transport.
</p><p>Liquefied natural gas occupies about 1/600th the volume of natural gas in the gaseous state at room temperature and atmospheric pressure. It is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odorless" title="Odorless">odorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">colorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">non-toxic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corrosive_substance" title="Corrosive substance">non-corrosive</a>. Hazards include flammability after vaporization into a gaseous state, freezing, and <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxia</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Liquefaction_of_gases" title="Liquefaction of gases">liquefaction</a> process involves removal of certain components, such as dust, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid_gases" title="Acid gases">acid gases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, water, and heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a>, which could cause difficulty downstream. The natural gas is then <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensed</a> into a liquid at close to atmospheric pressure (maximum transport pressure set at around 25 kPa or 3.6 psi) by cooling it to approximately −162 °C (111 K).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>LNG achieves a higher reduction in volume than <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG) so that the <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of LNG is 2.4 times greater than that of CNG or 60% that of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This makes LNG cost efficient to transport over long distances where pipelines do not exist. Specially designed <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenic</a> sea vessels (<a href="/wiki/LNG_carrier" title="LNG carrier">LNG carriers</a>) or cryogenic road tankers are used for its transport. Even if pressurized, methane must be cooled below its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_temperature" title="Critical temperature">critical temperature</a> of −82.3 °C (190.8 K) in order to be liquefied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>LNG, when it is not highly refined for special uses, is principally used for transporting natural gas to markets, where it is regasified and distributed as pipeline natural gas. It can be used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicles" title="Natural gas vehicles">LNG-fueled road vehicles</a>. 
However, it remains more common to design vehicles to use <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a>. As of  2002<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the relatively higher cost of LNG production and the need to store LNG in more expensive <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_storage_dewar" title="Cryogenic storage dewar">cryogenic tanks</a> had slowed widespread commercial use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas located far from its user base is often released into the atmosphere or <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">flared</a>. Some is subjected to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquid" title="Gas to liquid">gas to liquids</a> technologies (GTL) to produce liquid fuels, which are more readily transported than methane.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid-methane_rocket_fuel">Liquid-methane rocket fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Liquid-methane rocket fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Refined  liquid methane is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_rocket_propellants#Bipropellants" title="Liquid rocket propellants">used as</a> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel" title="Rocket fuel">rocket fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aiaa2004_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-aiaa2004-28">[28]</a></sup>  Methane is reported to offer the advantage over <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> of depositing less carbon on the internal parts of rocket motors, reducing the difficulty of re-use of boosters.
</p><p>Methane is abundant in many parts of the Solar system and potentially could be harvested on the surface of another solar-system body (in particular, using <a href="/wiki/In_situ_resource_utilization" title="In situ resource utilization">methane production from local materials</a> found on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zubrin20121215_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-zubrin20121215-29">[29]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>), providing fuel for a return journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aiaa2004_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-aiaa2004-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20070504_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20070504-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_feedstock">Chemical feedstock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Chemical feedstock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methane is converted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthesis_gas" title="Synthesis gas">synthesis gas</a>, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and hydrogen, by <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a>. This endergonic process (requiring energy) utilizes catalysts and requires high temperatures, around 700–1100 °C:
</p>
<dl><dd>CH<sub>4</sub>  + H<sub>2</sub>O   →   CO  +  3 H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Methane is also subjected to free-radical <a href="/wiki/Halogenation" title="Halogenation">chlorination</a> in the production of chloromethanes, although methanol is a more typical precursor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generation">Generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemistry" title="Biogeochemistry">Biogeochemistry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_sustainable_methane_fuel_production.PNG"><img alt="Diagram of sustainable methane fuel production.PNG" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="515" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Diagram_of_sustainable_methane_fuel_production.PNG/300px-Diagram_of_sustainable_methane_fuel_production.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Diagram_of_sustainable_methane_fuel_production.PNG/450px-Diagram_of_sustainable_methane_fuel_production.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Diagram_of_sustainable_methane_fuel_production.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_sustainable_methane_fuel_production.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_routes">Geological routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Geological routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are two main routes for geological methane generation, organic (thermogenic), and inorganic (<a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">abiotic</a>, meaning non-living). Thermally generated methane, referred to as thermogenic, originates from deeper sedimentary <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">strata</a>. Thermogenic methane (CH<sub>4</sub>) formation occurs due to the breakup of organic matter, forced by elevated temperatures and pressures. This type of methane is considered to be the primary methane type in sedimentary basins, and from an economical perspective the most important source of natural gas. Thermogenic methane components are generally considered to be relic (from an earlier time). Generally, formation of thermogenic methane (at depth) can occur through organic matter breakup, or organic synthesis. Both ways can involve microorganisms (methanogenesis) but may also occur inorganically. The involved anaerobic and aerobic processes can also consume methane, with and without microorganisms. The more important source of methane at depth (crystalline bedrock) is abiotic. Abiotic means that the methane formation took place involving inorganic compounds, without biological activity, magmatic or created at low temperatures and pressures through water-rock reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_routes">Biological routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Biological routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">methanogenesis</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring methane is mainly produced by microbial <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">methanogenesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This multistep process is used by microorganisms as an energy source. The net reaction is
</p>
<dl><dd>CO<sub>2</sub> + 8 H<sup>+</sup> + 8 e<sup>−</sup> → CH<sub>4</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>The final step in the process is catalyzed by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Coenzyme-B_sulfoethylthiotransferase" title="Coenzyme-B sulfoethylthiotransferase">Coenzyme-B sulfoethylthiotransferase</a>. Methanogenesis is a form of <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic respiration</a> used by organisms that occupy <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfill</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruminants" title="Ruminants">ruminants</a> (for example <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cows" title="Cows">cows</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>), and the guts of termites.
</p><p>It is uncertain whether plants are a source of methane emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_routes">Industrial routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Industrial routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are many technological methane production methods. Methane created from <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> in industrial plants via biological route is called <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>. A more synthetic method to produce methane is <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenating</a> carbon dioxide through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabatier_process" title="Sabatier process">Sabatier process</a>. Methane is also a side product of the hydrogenation of carbon monoxide in the <a href="/wiki/Fischer%E2%80%93Tropsch_process" title="Fischer–Tropsch process">Fischer–Tropsch process</a>, which is practiced on a large scale to produce longer-chain molecules than methane. Example of large-scale coal-to-methane gasification is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Plains_Synfuels" title="Great Plains Synfuels">Great Plains Synfuels</a> plant, started in 1984 in Beulah, North Dakota as a way to develop abundant local resources of low-grade <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a>, a resource that is otherwise very hard to transport for its weight, <a href="/wiki/Coal_assay#Ash" title="Coal assay">ash</a> content, low calorific value and propensity to <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_combustion" title="Spontaneous combustion">spontaneous combustion</a> during storage and transport.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_to_gas#Power_to_methane" title="Power to gas">Power to methane</a> is a technology that uses <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electrical power</a> to produce hydrogen from water by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> and uses the <a href="/wiki/Sabatier_reaction" title="Sabatier reaction">Sabatier reaction</a> to combine hydrogen with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> to produce methane. As of 2016, this is mostly under development and not in large-scale use. Theoretically, the process could be used as a buffer for excess and off-peak power generated by highly fluctuating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_generators" title="Wind generators">wind generators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar arrays</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">conversion efficiency</a> of power to methane is 49–65%, and full power–methane–power cycle is 30–38%.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_synthesis">Laboratory synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Laboratory synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although methane can in principle be produced by a variety of forcing methods, it is conveniently generated by protonation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methyl_lithium" title="Methyl lithium">methyl lithium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methylmagnesium_iodide" title="Methylmagnesium iodide">methylmagnesium iodide</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Methane was discovered and isolated by <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a> between 1776 and 1778 when studying <a href="/wiki/Marsh_gas" title="Marsh gas">marsh gas</a> from <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maggiore" title="Lake Maggiore">Lake Maggiore</a>. It is the major component of natural gas, about 87% by volume. The major source of methane is extraction from geological deposits known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_fields" title="Natural gas fields">natural gas fields</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_seam_gas" title="Coal seam gas">coal seam gas</a> extraction becoming a major source (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_bed_methane_extraction" title="Coal bed methane extraction">Coal bed methane extraction</a>, a method for extracting methane from a <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> deposit, while <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_coal_bed_methane_recovery" title="Enhanced coal bed methane recovery">enhanced coal bed methane recovery</a> is a method of recovering methane from non-mineable coal seams). It is associated with other <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> fuels, and sometimes accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>. Methane is produced at shallow levels (low pressure) by <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a> and reworked methane from deep under the Earth's surface. In general, the <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> that generate natural gas are buried deeper and at higher temperatures than those that contain <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a>.
</p><p>Methane is generally transported in bulk by <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipeline</a> in its natural gas form, or LNG carriers in its liquefied form; few countries transport it by truck.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_sources">Alternative sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Alternative sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="945" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg/220px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg/330px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg/440px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_1898_Testing_Sheep_for_Methane_Production.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Testing Australian sheep for exhaled methane production (2001), <a href="/wiki/CSIRO" title="CSIRO">CSIRO</a></div></div></div>
<p>Apart from gas fields, an alternative method of obtaining methane is via <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> generated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> of organic matter including <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>, wastewater sludge, <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a> (including landfills), or any other biodegradable feedstock, under anaerobic conditions. <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">Rice</a> fields also generate large amounts of methane during plant growth. <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">Methane hydrates/clathrates</a> (ice-like combinations of methane and water on the sea floor, found in vast quantities) are a potential future source of methane. Cattle belch methane accounts for 16% of the world's annual methane emissions to the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> One study reported that the livestock sector in general (primarily cattle, chickens, and pigs) produces 37% of all human-induced methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Early research has found a number of medical treatments and dietary adjustments that help slightly limit the production of methane in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruminants" title="Ruminants">ruminants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> A 2009 study found that at a conservative estimate, at least 51% of global greenhouse gas emissions were attributable to the life cycle and supply chain of livestock products, meaning all meat, dairy, and by-products, and their transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> More recently, a 2013 study estimated that livestock accounted for 44 percent of human-induced methane and 14.5 percent of human-induced greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Many efforts are underway to reduce livestock methane production and trap the gas to use as energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The state of California has been particularly active in this area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatology</a> research published in <i><a href="/wiki/Current_Biology" title="Current Biology">Current Biology</a></i> suggests that <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinosaurs" title="Dinosaurs">dinosaurs</a> may have warmed the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_methane">Atmospheric methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Atmospheric methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="Methane_in_Earth.27s_atmosphere"></span>
<span id="Methane_as_a_greenhouse_gas"></span>
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">Atmospheric methane</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="650" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png/300px-Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png/450px-Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png/600px-Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mlo_ch4_ts_obs_03437.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Methane concentrations up to May 2018 (Mauna Loa)</div></div></div>
<p>In 2010, methane levels in the Arctic were measured at 1850 nmol/mol.  This level is over twice as high as at any time in the last 400,000 years. Historic methane concentrations in the world's atmosphere have ranged between 300 and 400 nmol/mol during glacial periods commonly known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_ages" title="Ice ages">ice ages</a>, and between 600 and 700 nmol/mol during the warm <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial</a> periods. The Earth's oceans are a potential important source of Arctic methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane is an important <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> of 34 compared to CO<sub>2</sub> over a 100-year period, and 72 over a 20-year period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR5WG1_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-AR5WG1-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Earth's atmospheric methane concentration has increased by about 150% since 1750, and it accounts for 20% of the total <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> from all of the long-lived and globally mixed greenhouse gases (these gases don't include water vapor which is by far the largest component of the greenhouse effect).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical_summary_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Technical_summary-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clathrates">Clathrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Clathrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methane is essentially insoluble in water, but significant deposits of <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane clathrate</a> have been found under sediments on the ocean floors of Earth at large depths. Estimates consider up to 15,000 gigatonnes of carbon may be stored in the form of clathrates (hydrates) in the ocean floor, not accounting for abiotic methane, a relatively newly discovered source of methane, formed below the ocean floor, in the earth crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> It has been suggested, that today's methane emission regime from the ocean floor, is potentially similar to that during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PETM" title="PETM">PETM</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_methane_release" title="Arctic methane release">Arctic methane release</a> from <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> and methane clathrates is an expected consequence and further cause of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anaerobic_oxidation_of_methane">Anaerobic oxidation of methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Anaerobic oxidation of methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is a group of bacteria that drive methane oxidation with nitrite as the oxidant, the <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_oxidation_of_methane" title="Anaerobic oxidation of methane">anaerobic oxidation of methane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Methane is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nontoxic" title="Nontoxic">nontoxic</a>, yet it is extremely flammable and may form <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosive</a> mixtures with air. Methane is also an <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant_gas" title="Asphyxiant gas">asphyxiant</a> if the oxygen concentration is reduced to below about 16% by displacement, as most people can <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization#need_for_cabin_pressurisation" title="Cabin pressurization">tolerate a reduction from 21% to 16% without ill effects</a>. The concentration of methane at which asphyxiation risk becomes significant is much higher than the 5–15% concentration in a flammable or explosive mixture. Methane off-gas can penetrate the interiors of buildings near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfills" title="Landfills">landfills</a> and expose occupants to significant levels of methane. Some buildings have specially engineered recovery systems below their basements to actively capture this gas and vent it away from the building.
</p><p>Methane gas explosions are responsible for many deadly mining disasters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> A methane gas explosion was the cause of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Big_Branch_Mine_disaster" title="Upper Big Branch Mine disaster">Upper Big Branch coal mine disaster</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> on April 5, 2010, killing 29.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_methane">Extraterrestrial methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_atmosphere" title="Extraterrestrial atmosphere">extraterrestrial atmosphere</a></div>
<p>Methane has been detected or is believed to exist on all planets of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar system</a> and most of the larger moons. With the possible exception of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars_(planet)" title="Life on Mars (planet)">Mars</a>, it is believed to have come from <a href="/wiki/Abiogenic_petroleum_origin" title="Abiogenic petroleum origin">abiotic</a> processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20121102_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20121102-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1440" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/220px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/330px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/440px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>) on Mars – potential sources and sinks.</div></div></div>
<p>On June 7th of 2018, NASA disclosed in a press conference that its <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a> had documented seasonal fluctuations of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmospheric methane</a> levels on Mars. These fluctuations peaked at the end of the Martian summer at 0.6 parts per billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180607vid_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180607vid-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC-20180607_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPC-20180607-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180607_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180607-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180607_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180607-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608a_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608a-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608b_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608b-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180608c_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180608c-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane has been proposed as a possible rocket propellant on future Mars missions due in part to the possibility of synthesizing it on the planet by <a href="/wiki/In_situ_resource_utilization#Mars" title="In situ resource utilization">in situ resource utilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> An adaptation of the Sabatier methanation reaction may be used with a mixed catalyst bed and a <a href="/wiki/Water-gas_shift_reaction#Reverse_water-gas_shift" title="Water-gas shift reaction">reverse water-gas shift</a> in a single reactor to produce methane from the raw materials available on Mars, utilizing water from the Martian subsoil and carbon dioxide in the Martian atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zubrin20121215_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-zubrin20121215-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane could be produced by a non-biological process called <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">’'serpentinization<i></i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serpentinization_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-serpentinization-69">[a]</a></sup> involving water, carbon dioxide, and the mineral <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, which is known to be common on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olivine_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-olivine-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Methane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-serpentinization-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-serpentinization_69-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There are many <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite"><i>serpentinization</i></a> reactions. <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">Olivine</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Solid_solution" title="Solid solution">solid solution</a> between <a href="/wiki/Forsterite" title="Forsterite">forsterite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fayalite" title="Fayalite">fayalite</a> whose general formula is (Fe,Mg)<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>. The reaction producing methane from olivine can be written as: <i>Forsterite + Fayalite + Water + Carbonic acid → Serpentine + Magnetite + Methane </i>, or (in balanced form): 18 Mg<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub> + 6 Fe<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub> + 26 H<sub>2</sub>O + CO<sub>2</sub> → 12 Mg<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub> + 4 Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + CH<sub>4</sub> </span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
